\chapter{What Geometric Algebra Gave Us}
\label{chap:conclusion}
% в•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђ

\section{The journey}

You started with vectors and the dot product.  You now have:

\begin{center}
\renewcommand{\arraystretch}{1.5}\small
\begin{tabular}{@{}L{3cm} L{4cm} L{4cm}@{}}
\toprule
\textbf{Chapter} & \textbf{GA concept} & \textbf{Transformer insight} \\
\midrule
1--2 & Vectors, geometric product & Hidden states encode both alignment
  and plane information \\
3 & Bivectors & Layer rotations have principal planes, not just
  magnitudes \\
4 & Orthonormal frames & Whitening establishes the metric for valid GA \\
5 & Rotors & Layer transitions are rotations with explicit planes \\
6 & Versor decomposition & Grade-0 (stretch) vs.\ grade-2 (rotation) \\
7 & Principal planes & Rotation plane evolution across the network \\
8 & Eigenvalues & Grade-0 dominates control; late layers are highly
  selective \\
9 & Commutators & Non-commutativity lives in specific planes \\
10 & Holonomy & Curvature has a direction, not just a magnitude \\
11 & Capacity & Decompose computational complexity by plane \\
12 & Dependency + bivectors & Identify the planes that matter \\
13 & Applications & Diagnose and steer via plane-specific interventions \\
\bottomrule
\end{tabular}
\end{center}

The table above is a chapter-by-chapter inventory.  But the real
contribution is not a list --- it is a \emph{structure}.  The following
diagram shows how the pieces fit together into one coherent geometric
picture.

% в”Ђв”Ђ Figure: The geometry of transformer computation в”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђ
\begin{figure}[ht]
\centering
\begin{tikzpicture}[
  >=Stealth,
  quadrant/.style={draw=#1, fill=#1!6!white, rounded corners=6pt,
    line width=0.8pt, minimum width=5.2cm, minimum height=4.0cm,
    inner sep=6pt},
  qtitle/.style={font=\small\bfseries, #1},
  qlabel/.style={font=\footnotesize, DGray},
  qeq/.style={font=\small, #1},
  arr/.style={->, line width=1.4pt, Navy},
  arrlbl/.style={font=\scriptsize, Navy, fill=white, inner sep=2pt},
]

% в”Ђв”Ђ Top-left: Hidden-state grid (Ch 1) в”Ђв”Ђ
\begin{scope}[shift={(-4.2,3.0)}]
  \node[quadrant=Navy] (grid) at (0,0) {};
  \node[qtitle=Navy] at (0,1.5) {Hidden-state grid};
  \node[qlabel, text width=4.2cm, align=center] at (0,0.7)
    {Chapters 1, 4};

  % Mini grid
  \foreach \x in {0,...,3} {
    \foreach \y in {0,...,2} {
      \fill[Navy!40] (-1.6+\x*0.7, -0.9+\y*0.5) circle (2pt);
    }
  }
  % Axis labels
  \draw[->, DGray, line width=0.4pt] (-1.9,-1.1) -- (0.9,-1.1)
    node[right, font=\tiny, DGray] {$t$};
  \draw[->, DGray, line width=0.4pt] (-1.9,-1.1) -- (-1.9,0.3)
    node[above, font=\tiny, DGray] {$l$};

  \node[qeq=Navy] at (0, -1.5) {$H(l,t) \in \mathbb{R}^k$};
\end{scope}

% в”Ђв”Ђ Top-right: Geometric product (Ch 2вЂ“3) в”Ђв”Ђ
\begin{scope}[shift={(4.2,3.0)}]
  \node[quadrant=Purple] (prod) at (0,0) {};
  \node[qtitle=Purple] at (0,1.5) {Geometric product};
  \node[qlabel, text width=4.2cm, align=center] at (0,0.7)
    {Chapters 2, 3};

  % Mini vectors + parallelogram
  \draw[->, Navy, line width=1pt] (-0.8,-0.2) -- (0.8,-0.2)
    node[right, font=\tiny, Navy] {$a$};
  \draw[->, Blue, line width=1pt] (-0.8,-0.2) -- (0.0,0.6)
    node[above, font=\tiny, Blue] {$b$};
  \fill[Purple!15] (-0.8,-0.2) -- (0.8,-0.2) -- (1.6,0.6) -- (0.0,0.6)
    -- cycle;

  \node[qeq=Purple] at (0, -1.5)
    {$ab = \underset{\text{\tiny scalar}}{a \cdot b}
    + \underset{\text{\tiny plane}}{a \wedge b}$};
\end{scope}

% в”Ђв”Ђ Bottom-left: Rotor field (Ch 5вЂ“6) в”Ђв”Ђ
\begin{scope}[shift={(-4.2,-3.0)}]
  \node[quadrant=Teal] (rotor) at (0,0) {};
  \node[qtitle=Teal] at (0,1.5) {Rotor field};
  \node[qlabel, text width=4.2cm, align=center] at (0,0.7)
    {Chapters 5, 6};

  % Mini rotor circles with visible rotation arcs
  \foreach \i/\col in {0/Teal, 1/Purple, 2/DGreen, 3/Amber} {
    \draw[\col, line width=0.7pt]
      (-1.2+\i*0.8, -0.1) circle (0.25);
    \draw[->, \col, line width=0.8pt]
      ([shift={(60:0.40)}]-1.2+\i*0.8, -0.1)
      arc[start angle=60, end angle=300, radius=0.40];
  }

  \node[qeq=Teal] at (0, -1.5)
    {$R^{(l)}(t) = \exp(-B^{(l)}(t)/2)$};
\end{scope}

% в”Ђв”Ђ Bottom-right: Curvature / holonomy (Ch 9вЂ“10) в”Ђв”Ђ
\begin{scope}[shift={(4.2,-3.0)}]
  \node[quadrant=DRed] (curv) at (0,0) {};
  \node[qtitle=DRed] at (0,1.5) {Curvature \& holonomy};
  \node[qlabel, text width=4.2cm, align=center] at (0,0.7)
    {Chapters 9, 10, 11};

  % Mini loop вЂ” shifted down to avoid chapter label
  \draw[->, Teal, line width=1pt] (-0.6,-0.6) -- (0.4,-0.6);
  \draw[->, Teal, line width=1pt] (0.4,-0.6) -- (0.4,0.2);
  \draw[->, Purple, line width=1pt, densely dashed]
    (-0.6,-0.6) -- (-0.6,0.2);
  \draw[->, Purple, line width=1pt, densely dashed]
    (-0.6,0.2) -- (0.4,0.2);
  % Mismatch indicator вЂ” to the right of the loop
  \draw[DRed, line width=1.4pt, ->] (0.75,-0.35)
    arc[start angle=-40, end angle=40, radius=0.55]
    node[right=2pt, font=\scriptsize, DRed] {$\Delta\theta$};

  \node[qeq=DRed] at (0, -1.5)
    {$\Omega(l,t) = \log R_{\text{loop}}(l,t)$};
\end{scope}

% в”Ђв”Ђ Central connecting arrows в”Ђв”Ђ
% Grid в†’ Product (top)
\draw[arr] (grid.east) -- (prod.west)
  node[arrlbl, midway, above] {pairwise relationships};

% Grid в†’ Rotor (left)
\draw[arr] (grid.south) -- (rotor.north)
  node[arrlbl, midway, left] {layer transitions};

% Product в†’ Rotor (diagonal) вЂ” label in the gap, anchored left
\draw[arr] (prod.south west) -- (rotor.north east);
\node[font=\scriptsize, Navy, fill=white, inner sep=2pt,
  rotate=-27, anchor=west] at (2.2, 0.55) {bivectors $\to$ rotors};

% Rotor в†’ Curvature (bottom) вЂ” label below the line to avoid center box
\draw[arr] (rotor.east) -- (curv.west)
  node[arrlbl, midway, below=2pt] {compose around loops};

% Product в†’ Curvature (right)
\draw[arr] (prod.south) -- (curv.north)
  node[arrlbl, midway, right] {commutators};

% в”Ђв”Ђ Central summary в”Ђв”Ђ
\node[draw=Navy, fill=LBlue, rounded corners=4pt,
  inner sep=5pt, font=\small, text width=3.8cm, align=center]
  at (0,0) {%
  $H : (l,t) \to \mathbb{R}^k$\\[2pt]
  $R : (l,t) \to \mathrm{Spin}(k)$\\[2pt]
  $\Omega : (l,t) \to \mathfrak{so}(k)$\\[4pt]
  {\footnotesize\textbf{vectors\,,\;rotations\,,\;curvature}}};

\end{tikzpicture}

\caption{The geometric structure of transformer computation.
Hidden states form a vector field $H(l,t)$ over a grid of layers and
token positions.  Relationships between vectors are captured by the
geometric product, which decomposes into scalar (alignment) and
bivector (plane) components.  Each layer induces a
position-dependent transformation expressed as a rotor field
$R^{(l)}(t)$.  When these transformations do not commute across
directions, parallel transport around loops produces holonomy,
captured by the curvature bivector $\Omega(l,t)$.  Together, these
elements describe the transformer as a geometric system evolving
across both depth and context.}
\label{fig:master-geometry}
\end{figure}

\begin{keyidea}
This framework unifies the analysis of transformers into a single
geometric picture.  Instead of viewing the model as a sequence of
matrix operations, we interpret it as a field of vectors evolving
under local rotations, with global structure revealed through curvature.
The grid provides the domain, bivectors reveal directional structure,
rotors describe transformations, and holonomy measures interaction
between directions.  This perspective exposes structure that is invisible
to scalar summaries and traditional linear analysis.

\medskip
\centering
\emph{Learning = discovering geometric structure in the hidden-state field.}
\end{keyidea}


% в”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђ
\section{The transformer as a discrete geometric field theory}
\label{sec:field-theory}
% в”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђ

The observations throughout this book --- hidden-state grids, bivector
decompositions, rotor fields, holonomy --- are not independent facts.
They are elements of a single structure.  This section formalises
that structure.

\subsection{The base space: a discrete lattice}

Define the grid
\[
  \mathcal{G} = \{(l,t) \mid l = 0,\dots,L-1,\; t = 0,\dots,T-1\}.
\]
The vertical axis indexes layers (depth, computation time); the
horizontal axis indexes token positions (sequence context).
$\mathcal{G}$ is the \textbf{base space} of the system.

\subsection{The fiber: hidden-state space}

At each grid point, the model maintains a vector
$H(l,t) \in \mathbb{R}^k$ (after whitening, so the metric is
Euclidean).  Thus the transformer defines a \textbf{vector field}:
\[
  H : \mathcal{G} \longrightarrow \mathbb{R}^k.
\]
In the language of differential geometry, this is a section of a
trivial fiber bundle with base $\mathcal{G}$ and fiber
$\mathbb{R}^k$.

\subsection{Local transformations as a connection}

Moving across the grid changes the hidden state.  Each direction
has its own transport operator.

\paragraph{Vertical direction (layers).}
A layer induces a transformation
$U^{(l)}(t) : H(l,t) \to H(l+1,t)$,
locally approximated as
\[
  U^{(l)}(t) \;\approx\; \exp\!\big(B^{(l)}(t)\big),
  \qquad B^{(l)}(t) \in \mathfrak{so}(k).
\]
The generator $B^{(l)}(t)$ is a skew-symmetric operator --- a
bivector encoding the rotation plane and magnitude.

\paragraph{Horizontal direction (tokens).}
Attention induces transformations across token positions:
$T^{(l)}(t) : H(l,t) \to H(l,t+1)$,
locally approximated as
\[
  T^{(l)}(t) \;\approx\; \exp\!\big(C^{(l)}(t)\big),
  \qquad C^{(l)}(t) \in \mathfrak{so}(k).
\]

Together, $U^{(l)}$ and $T^{(l)}$ define a \textbf{connection} on the
grid: they prescribe how vectors are transported along vertical and
horizontal directions.

\subsection{Rotor field representation}

Using GA, the local transformations become rotors:
\[
  R^{(l)}(t) = \exp\!\left(-\tfrac{1}{2}\, B^{(l)}(t)\right),
  \qquad
  H(l+1,t) \;\approx\; R^{(l)}(t)\; H(l,t)\; \widetilde{R}^{(l)}(t),
\]
where $\widetilde{R}$ is the reverse (inverse for rotors).  The
map $R^{(l)} : t \mapsto \mathrm{Spin}(k)$ is a field from token
positions to the spin group: a transformer layer defines a
\emph{field of rotations}, not a single transformation.

\subsection{Curvature from non-commutativity}

Consider a loop on the grid:
$(l,t) \to (l,t\!+\!1) \to (l\!+\!1,t\!+\!1) \to (l\!+\!1,t) \to (l,t)$.
The loop operator is
\[
  R_{\text{loop}}(l,t) \;=\;
  R_{\uparrow}^{(t+1)}\;\;
  R_{\rightarrow}^{(l+1)}\;\;
  \big(R_{\uparrow}^{(t)}\big)^{-1}\;\;
  \big(R_{\rightarrow}^{(l)}\big)^{-1}.
\]
If the vertical and horizontal transformations commute,
$R_{\text{loop}} = I$ and the grid is flat.  Otherwise the
\textbf{curvature bivector}
\[
  \Omega(l,t) = \log R_{\text{loop}}(l,t) \;\in\; \mathfrak{so}(k)
\]
is non-zero.  For small transformations, the holonomy is governed by
the commutator:
\[
  R_{\text{loop}} \;\approx\; \exp\!\big([B^{(l)}, C^{(l)}]\big),
  \qquad
  \Omega(l,t) \;\approx\; [B^{(l)}(t),\; C^{(l)}(t)].
\]

\begin{gabox}
\textbf{Curvature arises from non-commutativity of transformations.}
When layer and token operations commute, curvature is zero and the
computation is separable.  When they do not commute, curvature
appears --- and its bivector reveals \emph{which plane} of
representation space is curved.
\end{gabox}

\subsection{Interpretation for transformer computation}

This framework yields five concrete re-interpretations:

\begin{enumerate}[nosep]
\item \textbf{Tokens are positions} in a structured space, not
  independent objects.
\item \textbf{Hidden states are fields} --- contextualised vectors
  evolving across the grid.
\item \textbf{Layers are local transformations} --- position-dependent,
  not uniform.
\item \textbf{Attention induces horizontal transport} --- moving
  information across token positions.
\item \textbf{Reasoning corresponds to curvature} --- it appears when
  updates cannot be decomposed into independent layer and token steps.
\end{enumerate}

\subsection{Summary of the formal structure}

The full framework is captured in three fields:
\[
\begin{aligned}
  H &: \mathcal{G} \;\longrightarrow\; \mathbb{R}^k
    &&\text{(hidden-state field)} \\[3pt]
  R &: \mathcal{G} \;\longrightarrow\; \mathrm{Spin}(k)
    &&\text{(rotor field)} \\[3pt]
  \Omega &: \mathcal{G} \;\longrightarrow\; \mathfrak{so}(k)
    &&\text{(curvature field)}
\end{aligned}
\]

\begin{keyidea}
A transformer is a discrete geometric field system whose computation
is governed by rotations and curvature.  The grid provides the domain,
bivectors reveal directional structure, rotors describe
transformations, and holonomy measures interaction between directions.
\end{keyidea}

\section{What was invisible before}

Scalar summaries --- the size of a rotation ($\|U - I\|_F$), the size of a commutator ($\|[A_i, A_j]\|_F$), or the curvature magnitude ($\|\Omega - I\|_F$) --- tell you \emph{how much}.  GA tells you \emph{how much} and
\emph{in which direction}.  Specifically:

\begin{enumerate}[nosep]
\item Which planes each layer rotates in.
\item Which planes the curvature acts in.
\item Which planes different layers fail to commute in.
\item Which planes the model's output depends on.
\end{enumerate}

None of this information is accessible from norms alone.  It requires
the full bivector.

\section{Where to go next}

\begin{enumerate}
\item \textbf{Higher grades.}  We used only grades 0 and 2.  Do
  trivectors (grade 3) carry meaningful information about three-layer
  interactions?

\item \textbf{Conformal GA.}  Conformal GA extends the algebra by adding two extra dimensions (written $\mathrm{Cl}(k+1,1)$), which allows translations --- not just rotations --- to be represented algebraically.  Could this unify residual
  connections (translations) with rotations (rotors)?

\item \textbf{GA in training dynamics.}  How does the bivector field
  evolve during pre-training?  Do specific planes emerge at specific
  training stages?

\item \textbf{Multimodal models.}  When vision and language tokens
  interact, do they occupy different rotation planes?

\item \textbf{Projective GA.}  Projective GA (written $\mathrm{Cl}(k,0,1)$) adds one extra dimension whose square is zero, providing a natural way to represent the affine shifts that residual connections add.
\end{enumerate}

\section{Further reading}

\begin{itemize}[nosep]
\item Doran and Lasenby, \emph{Geometric Algebra for Physicists}
  (Cambridge, 2003).  The most accessible GA introduction.
\item Dorst, Fontijne, and Mann, \emph{Geometric Algebra for Computer
  Science} (Morgan Kaufmann, 2007).  Practical and code-oriented.
\item Lounesto, \emph{Clifford Algebras and Spinors} (Cambridge, 2001).
  The mathematical reference.
\item Sudjianto and Zhang, \emph{Layer--Time Geometry: How Language
  Models Process Information} (2026).  The first-edition linear algebra
  treatment.
\item Fang, Li, and Sudjianto, \emph{Design and Modeling for Computer
  Experiments} (Chapman \& Hall/CRC, 2006).  Background on DOE
  methodology.
\end{itemize}

% в•‘                                 PART V                                     в•‘
% в•љв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ќ
\part{Autoregressive Generation as Causal Frontier Geometry}
\label{part:frontier}


% в•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђ
\chapter{The Decode Frontier}
\label{chap:frontier}
% в•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђ

\begin{whycare}
Everything in Parts~I--IV analyses a transformer processing a
\emph{fixed} prompt --- the layer--token grid is static.
But real language models \emph{generate}: they extend the prompt one
token at a time, each new token feeding back into the model.
The geometric field is no longer a fixed rectangle; it grows a new
column at every decode step.
Understanding this expanding geometry is essential if GA is to say
anything about how models actually produce text.
\end{whycare}


% в”Ђв”Ђ 15.1 в”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђ
\section{Why the static view is incomplete}
\label{sec:frontier-static}

Chapters~\ref{chap:versor}--\ref{chap:dependency} treat the
hidden-state field $H^{(l)}_t$ as a fixed $(L+1) \times T$ matrix.
That picture is correct for a single forward pass, but it hides
three things about generation:

\begin{enumerate}[nosep]
\item \textbf{The grid grows.}  At decode step~$s$ the grid has
  $T_0 + s$ columns, where $T_0$ is the prompt length.
\item \textbf{Only the newest column is new.}  With the KV cache,
  earlier columns are unchanged; only the rightmost column ---
  the \textbf{frontier} --- carries fresh information.
\item \textbf{The frontier feeds back.}  The logits produced from
  the frontier determine the next token, which becomes the
  frontier at step~$s+1$.  Generation is a causal, recurrent
  process.
\end{enumerate}

\begin{transformer}
During autoregressive generation, a transformer with a KV cache
processes only the \emph{new} token at each step.  The key and value
tensors for all previous tokens are stored and reused.
For geometry, this means the hidden-state columns $H^{(l)}_{t}$ for
$t < T_0 + s$ are fixed once computed; only the frontier column
$H^{(l)}_{T_0+s}$ changes from step to step.
The expanding grid is therefore fully characterised by the sequence
of frontier columns.
\end{transformer}


% в”Ђв”Ђ 15.2 в”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђ
\section{The decode-step index}
\label{sec:frontier-index}

We augment the layer--token grid with a third index: the
\textbf{decode step} $s = 0, 1, 2, \ldots$

\begin{itemize}[nosep]
\item $s = 0$: the model processes the full prompt.  The frontier
  is the last prompt token.
\item $s \geq 1$: the model generates token~$s$.  The frontier is
  the hidden state at that token across all layers.
\end{itemize}

\paragraph{Notation.}
Let $\hat{H}^{(l)}_s \in \mathbb{R}^k$ denote the whitened
frontier hidden state at layer~$l$ and decode step~$s$.
The full frontier at step~$s$ is the column vector
$\hat{H}_s = [\hat{H}^{(1)}_s, \ldots, \hat{H}^{(L)}_s]$.
We skip layer~0 (the embedding) as in earlier chapters.

\begin{gabox}
\textbf{The frontier as a moving column.}
In the language of the layer--token grid (Chapter~\ref{chap:holonomy}),
generation appends one column per step.
Each new column's hidden states pass through the same sequence of
layer transitions, producing a new set of rotors, bivectors, and
curvature values.  The frontier is the rightmost edge of the
expanding grid --- the boundary where geometry is actively being
created.
\end{gabox}


% в”Ђв”Ђ 15.3 в”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђ
\section{Frontier rotation angle}
\label{sec:frontier-angle}

At each layer transition $(l \to l+1)$ and decode step~$s$, we
measure how much the hidden state rotates:

\[
  \boxed{%
    \theta^{(l)}_s
    = \arccos\!\Bigl(
        \frac{\hat{H}^{(l)}_s \cdot \hat{H}^{(l+1)}_s}
             {\|\hat{H}^{(l)}_s\|\;\|\hat{H}^{(l+1)}_s\|}
      \Bigr)
  }
\]

This is identical to the rotation angle from
Chapter~\ref{chap:rotors}, but now it varies across decode
steps~$s$ as well as layers~$l$.  The result is a 2D field:
$\theta^{(l)}_s$ for $l = 1, \ldots, L-1$ and $s = 0, \ldots, S$.

\paragraph{What the data shows.}
Figure~\ref{fig:frontier-angles} shows the frontier angle heatmaps
for our three standard prompts (Qwen2.5-7B, greedy decoding, 50
generation steps).

\begin{figure}[ht]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=\linewidth]{figures_ga_learning/ch15_frontier_angles_3prompts.pdf}
\caption{\textbf{Frontier rotation angle heatmaps} ($\theta^{(l)}_s$,
layers $\times$ decode steps) for the three standard prompts.
All three show a bright band near layer~25, matching the static
peak from Chapter~\ref{chap:rotors}.  The Retrieval prompt
maintains a stable horizontal band; the Adversarial prompt
develops periodic vertical stripes once repetition begins.}
\label{fig:frontier-angles}
\end{figure}

\begin{finding}
The frontier angle field reveals two key patterns:

\textbf{Layer structure is preserved.}
All three prompts peak at layer~25 of~26 transitions, consistent
with the static analysis.  Mean frontier angles are
$0.59$~rad (Retrieval), $0.44$~rad (Reasoning), and
$0.56$~rad (Adversarial).

\textbf{Decode structure is prompt-dependent.}
The Retrieval prompt shows a stable horizontal band --- the model
applies similar rotations at each step as it retrieves factual
continuations.  The Reasoning prompt shows lower overall
angles, suggesting more conservative updates.
The Adversarial prompt develops a striking 9-step periodic pattern
once it collapses into the repetition
``\,\texttt{. I want to know how to do it}\,'' (9~tokens per cycle).
\end{finding}

\begin{figure}[ht]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=\linewidth]{figures_ga_learning/ch15_frontier_angle_summary_3prompts.pdf}
\caption{\textbf{Layer-averaged frontier angle profiles.}
Each curve averages $\theta^{(l)}_s$ across all decode steps at
each layer.  The three prompts share the same peak at layer~25
but differ in magnitude and shape.}
\label{fig:frontier-angle-summary}
\end{figure}

\begin{analogy}
Think of a river carving through rock.  The static view is a
photograph of the canyon at one moment.  The frontier view is a
time-lapse showing the river's edge advancing.  The canyon walls
(layer structure) stay fixed, but the cutting edge (decode frontier)
reveals how fast and where the river is deepening.
\end{analogy}


% в”Ђв”Ђ 15.4 в”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђ
\section{Cayley bivector at the frontier}
\label{sec:frontier-cayley}

The rotation angle $\theta^{(l)}_s$ tells us \emph{how much} the
hidden state rotates, but not \emph{in which plane}.  To recover the
plane, we use the \textbf{Cayley bivector} --- a closed-form
skew-symmetric matrix that avoids the costly matrix logarithm.

\begin{keyidea}
For unit vectors $u$ and $v$ in $\mathbb{R}^k$, the Cayley bivector
is:
\[
  A(u, v) = \frac{v\,u^\top - u\,v^\top}{1 + v^\top u}
\]

This is a rank-2 skew-symmetric matrix --- hence a bivector in
$\mathrm{Cl}(k,0)$.  It satisfies $\exp(A) \, u = v$ to first
order and is exact when $u$ and $v$ span a 2D subspace.
Unlike the Rodrigues rotor of Chapter~\ref{chap:rotors}, which
requires $\log R$ via a matrix logarithm, the Cayley form is a
single closed-form expression.  This makes it practical for the
frontier, where we must compute one bivector per layer per decode
step (thousands of bivectors for a typical generation).
\end{keyidea}

At each layer transition and decode step, we compute:
\[
  A^{(l)}_s
  = \frac{
      \hat{u}^{(l+1)}_s \bigl(\hat{u}^{(l)}_s\bigr)^\top
      - \hat{u}^{(l)}_s \bigl(\hat{u}^{(l+1)}_s\bigr)^\top
    }{
      1 + \bigl(\hat{u}^{(l+1)}_s\bigr)^\top \hat{u}^{(l)}_s
    }
\]

where $\hat{u}^{(l)}_s = \hat{H}^{(l)}_s / \|\hat{H}^{(l)}_s\|$
is the unit-normalised frontier vector.

\begin{gabox}
\textbf{Cayley vs.\ Rodrigues.}
Chapter~\ref{chap:rotors} extracted the bivector generator via
$B = \log R$ where $R$ is the orthogonal factor from polar
decomposition.  The Rodrigues approach is exact for the full
transformation but requires scipy's \texttt{logm}, which costs
$O(k^3)$ per call.  The Cayley form trades the full-rank accuracy
for closed-form speed: it captures the dominant rotation plane
between two vectors without decomposing the full layer operator.
For frontier analysis, where we need $\sim\!L \times S$ bivectors
(e.g., $26 \times 51 = 1{,}326$ for Qwen2.5-7B generating 50
tokens), Cayley is the practical choice.
\end{gabox}

\begin{pitfall}
The Cayley bivector $A(u,v)$ is a rank-2 matrix regardless of
the ambient dimension~$k$.  It captures only the \emph{dominant}
rotation plane between $u$ and $v$.  If the actual layer
transformation is a compound rotation in multiple planes (as
Chapter~\ref{chap:versor} showed), the Cayley bivector misses the
subdominant planes.  For frontier analysis this is acceptable:
we are tracking the \emph{dominant} geometric signal at each step,
not reconstructing the full layer operator.
\end{pitfall}


% в”Ђв”Ђ 15.5 в”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђ
\section{Incremental bivector and commutator spikes}
\label{sec:frontier-commutator}

With a bivector at every $(l, s)$ grid point, we can track how the
rotation plane \emph{changes} from one decode step to the next.

\paragraph{Incremental bivector.}
The step-to-step change is:
\[
  \Delta A^{(l)}_s = A^{(l)}_s - A^{(l)}_{s-1}
\]

Its Frobenius norm $\|\Delta A^{(l)}_s\|_F$ measures how much the
rotation plane has shifted at layer~$l$ between steps $s-1$
and~$s$.

\paragraph{Commutator.}
As in Chapter~\ref{chap:commutators}, the commutator of successive
bivectors detects when the rotation plane has genuinely
\emph{reoriented} (not just changed magnitude):
\[
  [A^{(l)}_s,\, A^{(l)}_{s-1}]
  = A^{(l)}_s A^{(l)}_{s-1} - A^{(l)}_{s-1} A^{(l)}_s
\]

If the model rotates in the same plane at steps $s-1$ and $s$,
the commutator vanishes.  A nonzero commutator means the model has
switched to a geometrically distinct rotation plane --- a
\textbf{reorientation event}.

\paragraph{What the data shows.}

\begin{figure}[ht]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=\linewidth]{figures_ga_learning/ch15_commutator_spikes_3prompts.pdf}
\caption{\textbf{Commutator spikes at the frontier.}
Layer-averaged commutator norm $\|[A^{(l)}_s, A^{(l)}_{s-1}]\|_F$
at each decode step, with token labels.  Spikes mark reorientation
events --- tokens where the model switches rotation plane.
The Adversarial prompt shows periodic spikes at the cycle boundaries
of its 9-token repetition.}
\label{fig:commutator-spikes}
\end{figure}

\begin{finding}
Commutator norms reveal the ``surprise'' structure of generation:

\begin{itemize}[nosep]
\item \textbf{Retrieval} (mean $\|[\cdot]\| = 0.10$):
  moderate, with spikes at content-bearing tokens (dates, numbers,
  proper nouns).
\item \textbf{Reasoning} (mean $\|[\cdot]\| = 0.05$):
  lowest overall --- the model maintains a consistent rotation plane
  through the deductive chain.
\item \textbf{Adversarial} (mean $\|[\cdot]\| = 0.08$):
  periodic spikes at 9-step intervals, coinciding with the
  boundaries of the repeating phrase.  Each cycle restart forces a
  brief reorientation before the model re-enters the same orbit.
\end{itemize}

The commutator norm acts as a \emph{geometric surprise} signal:
it is large precisely when the next token requires the model to
rotate in a new direction.
\end{finding}


% в”Ђв”Ђ 15.6 в”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђ
\section{Plane drift: stability of the rotation plane}
\label{sec:frontier-drift}

A complementary view comes from measuring how stable the rotation
plane is across decode steps.  Define the \textbf{plane drift}:
\[
  \boxed{%
    d^{(l)}_s = 1 - \bigl|\cos(A^{(l)}_s,\, A^{(l)}_{s-1})\bigr|
  }
\]

where the cosine is the Frobenius inner product normalised by the
Frobenius norms:
\[
  \cos(A, B) = \frac{\langle A, B \rangle_F}{\|A\|_F\;\|B\|_F}
  = \frac{\mathrm{tr}(A^\top B)}{\|A\|_F\;\|B\|_F}
\]

Drift $= 0$ means the plane is perfectly preserved; drift $= 1$
means the successive bivectors are orthogonal (maximally different
planes).

\begin{figure}[ht]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=\linewidth]{figures_ga_learning/ch15_plane_drift_3prompts.pdf}
\caption{\textbf{Plane drift at the frontier.}
Layer-averaged drift $d^{(l)}_s$ across decode steps.
All three prompts show high drift (mean $> 0.65$), indicating that
the rotation plane changes substantially from step to step.
The Reasoning prompt has the highest mean drift ($0.72$), consistent
with each token in a deductive chain requiring a distinct
geometric direction.}
\label{fig:plane-drift}
\end{figure}

\begin{finding}
Plane drift is high for all prompts: $0.66$ (Retrieval), $0.72$
(Reasoning), $0.68$ (Adversarial).
This means the model's rotation plane at each layer is
\emph{not} preserved across decode steps --- even when the rotation
angle is stable (as in the Retrieval prompt), the direction of
rotation shifts.

\textbf{Interpretation.}
High drift with low commutator (Reasoning) means the plane rotates
smoothly --- small, continuous adjustments.  High drift with periodic
commutator spikes (Adversarial) means the plane snaps between
discrete orientations at cycle boundaries.
\end{finding}

\begin{analogy}
A figure skater spinning at constant speed (stable angle) can still
reorient the axis of rotation --- tilting from vertical to
horizontal.  The rotation speed stays the same, but the plane
changes.  Plane drift measures this tilt; the commutator detects
when the tilt happens abruptly.
\end{analogy}


% в”Ђв”Ђ 15.7 в”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђ
\section{Gauge consistency: the fixed whitening metric}
\label{sec:frontier-gauge}

A critical design choice underlies all frontier computations: the
whitening metric is computed \textbf{once from the prompt} and then
applied to every decode step.

\begin{keyidea}
The whitening metric $\Sigma^{-1/2}$ (Chapter~\ref{chap:frames})
is estimated from the prompt's full hidden states --- all layers,
all prompt tokens.  This gives a fixed coordinate frame.  Every
frontier column is then projected into this frame:
\[
  \hat{H}^{(l)}_s = \Sigma^{-1/2}\bigl(H^{(l)}_s - \mu\bigr)
\]

where $\mu$ and $\Sigma$ are the mean and covariance of the
prompt hidden states.  The whitening dimension is $k = 256$.
\end{keyidea}

\paragraph{Why fix the metric?}

If we re-estimated the metric at each decode step (incorporating the
new token), the coordinate frame would shift.  Quantities like
$\theta^{(l)}_s$ and $A^{(l)}_s$ at different steps would live in
different gauges, making step-to-step comparisons meaningless.

By fixing the metric to the prompt, we ensure:

\begin{enumerate}[nosep]
\item All frontier vectors live in the same $k$-dimensional space.
\item Angles, bivectors, and commutators are directly comparable
  across steps.
\item The prompt serves as a \textbf{reference geometry} against
  which generation is measured.
\end{enumerate}

\begin{pitfall}
If the generated text drifts far from the prompt's distribution
(e.g., a code model suddenly generating poetry), the fixed metric
may not capture the relevant directions.  In practice, for short
generations ($S \leq 100$), the prompt metric is adequate.
For very long generations, a sliding-window metric is an open
research direction.
\end{pitfall}


% в”Ђв”Ђ 15.8 в”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђ
\section{Frontier holonomy}
\label{sec:frontier-holonomy}

The holonomy of Chapter~\ref{chap:holonomy} measures curvature
at each plaquette of the layer--token grid.  At the frontier,
each decode step creates a new column of plaquettes.

\paragraph{Construction.}
At decode step $s \geq 1$, we form a \emph{mini-grid} from
the previous frontier column ($s-1$) and the current one ($s$):
\[
  G_s = \bigl[\hat{H}_{s-1},\; \hat{H}_s\bigr]
  \qquad \text{shape: } (L, 2, k)
\]

Each layer transition in this mini-grid defines one plaquette.
The holonomy rotor around each plaquette gives the
\textbf{frontier curvature} $\mathcal{K}^{(l)}_s$.

\begin{figure}[ht]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=\linewidth]{figures_ga_learning/ch15_frontier_holonomy_3prompts.pdf}
\caption{\textbf{Frontier holonomy heatmaps.}
Scalar curvature $\mathcal{K}^{(l)}_s$ at frontier plaquettes
(layers $\times$ decode steps).  The Retrieval prompt shows
uniformly high curvature.  The Reasoning prompt has lower but
more structured curvature.  The Adversarial prompt shows
periodic banding at the 9-token cycle.}
\label{fig:frontier-holonomy}
\end{figure}

\begin{finding}
Mean frontier curvature:
$0.89$ (Retrieval), $0.66$ (Reasoning), $0.81$ (Adversarial).

These values are higher than the interior curvature from
Chapter~\ref{chap:holonomy}, which makes physical sense: the
frontier is where new information enters the model's representation,
creating stronger geometric distortion than the settled interior.

\textbf{The frontier is the high-curvature boundary of the
expanding grid.}
\end{finding}

\begin{figure}[ht]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=\linewidth]{figures_ga_learning/ch15_cumulative_curvature_3prompts.pdf}
\caption{\textbf{Cumulative frontier curvature.}
Running sum of layer-averaged curvature across decode steps.
Retrieval accumulates curvature fastest (steepest slope);
Reasoning accumulates slowest.  The Adversarial curve shows
a steady staircase pattern reflecting its periodic cycle.}
\label{fig:cumulative-curvature}
\end{figure}


% в”Ђв”Ђ 15.9 в”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђ
\section{Repetition collapse: a geometric signature}
\label{sec:frontier-repetition}

The Adversarial prompt provides a natural case study of generation
failure.  After the prompt, the model immediately collapses into the
9-token cycle ``\texttt{. I want to know how to do it}'' repeated
indefinitely.

\begin{figure}[ht]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=\linewidth]{figures_ga_learning/ch15_token_frontier_summary_3prompts.pdf}
\caption{\textbf{Per-token frontier summary.}
Layer-averaged frontier angle (top) and commutator norm (bottom)
at each decode step, annotated with the generated token.
The Adversarial prompt shows the geometric fingerprint of
repetition: a fixed 9-step pattern in both angle and commutator.}
\label{fig:token-frontier-summary}
\end{figure}

\begin{finding}
Repetition has a clear geometric fingerprint:

\begin{enumerate}[nosep]
\item \textbf{Periodic angle.}  The frontier angle at each layer
  repeats with a period equal to the cycle length (9 tokens).
  Each position in the cycle has a characteristic angle profile.
\item \textbf{Periodic commutator.}  Commutator spikes recur at
  the same relative positions within each cycle (typically at the
  cycle boundary, where the period marker ``.'' resets the
  sentence).
\item \textbf{Constant cumulative curvature rate.}
  The cumulative curvature curve becomes a straight line during
  repetition: each cycle adds the same total curvature as the
  previous one.  There is no new geometric information.
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Geometric criterion for repetition:}
a generation has collapsed into repetition when the frontier
angle profile and commutator profile become periodic with period
$P$, and the cumulative curvature becomes linear.  This criterion
does not require looking at the tokens themselves --- it is purely
geometric.
\end{finding}

\begin{keyidea}
Repetition is a \textbf{geometric fixed orbit}: the frontier traces
the same closed path in the GA field over and over.
The model's rotor field has collapsed from a complex, evolving
pattern to a simple periodic attractor.  Every cycle applies the
same sequence of rotors, producing the same bivectors, the same
commutators, and the same curvature.

This gives a GA-native definition of repetition that does not
depend on string matching.  A generation is \emph{geometrically
novel} when the frontier angle, commutator, and curvature at
step~$s$ cannot be predicted from the values at step~$s - P$ for
any fixed period~$P$.
\end{keyidea}


% в”Ђв”Ђ 15.10 в”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђ
\section{Code: running frontier analysis}
\label{sec:frontier-code}

The \texttt{layer\_time\_ga.generation} module implements all
frontier computations.  Here is the complete pipeline:

\begin{lstlisting}[caption={Frontier analysis pipeline}]
from layer_time_ga import (
    generate_with_frontier,
    whiten_frontier,
    compute_frontier_ga,
    frontier_holonomy,
)
from transformers import AutoModelForCausalLM, AutoTokenizer

# Load model
model = AutoModelForCausalLM.from_pretrained(
    "Qwen/Qwen2.5-7B",
    torch_dtype=torch.float16,
    device_map="auto",
)
tokenizer = AutoTokenizer.from_pretrained("Qwen/Qwen2.5-7B")

# Generate with frontier extraction
result = generate_with_frontier(
    model, tokenizer,
    prompt="The Eiffel Tower is ...",
    n_steps=50,
    temperature=0.0,   # greedy decoding
)

# Whiten using fixed prompt metric
result = whiten_frontier(result, whiten_components=256)

# Compute GA quantities
ga = compute_frontier_ga(result)
# ga.angles:           (26, 51) frontier rotation angles
# ga.commutator_norms: (26, 50) commutator norms
# ga.plane_drift:      (26, 50) plane drift values

# Frontier holonomy (GPU-accelerated)
holo = frontier_holonomy(result, device="cuda")
# holo: (26, 51) scalar curvature at frontier
\end{lstlisting}

\paragraph{Performance.}
The main cost is the autoregressive generation itself.
\texttt{compute\_frontier\_ga} uses the closed-form Cayley
bivector and vectorised \texttt{numpy} operations (no
\texttt{scipy.linalg.logm}), making it fast even for long
generations.  \texttt{frontier\_holonomy} delegates to the
GPU-batched holonomy computation from the backend.
For Qwen2.5-7B generating 50 tokens, the full pipeline
(generation + whitening + GA + holonomy) completes in under
two minutes on a single GPU.


% в”Ђв”Ђ 15.11 в”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђ
\section{Summary and bridge to the next chapter}
\label{sec:frontier-summary}

This chapter introduced the \textbf{decode frontier} --- the
rightmost column of the expanding layer--token grid during
autoregressive generation.  By fixing the whitening metric to the
prompt and extracting the frontier at each decode step, we
extended the static GA framework to generation:

\begin{center}
\renewcommand{\arraystretch}{1.4}\small
\begin{tabular}{@{}L{3.2cm} L{4.5cm} L{4.5cm}@{}}
\toprule
\textbf{Quantity} & \textbf{Static (Ch.\,6--12)} &
  \textbf{Frontier (this chapter)} \\
\midrule
Rotation angle &
  $\theta^{(l)}$, one per layer &
  $\theta^{(l)}_s$, one per layer per step \\
Bivector &
  $\bivec{l}$ via $\log R$ (Rodrigues) &
  $A^{(l)}_s$ via Cayley (closed-form) \\
Commutator &
  $[\bivec{l}, \bivec{l+1}]$ (layer--layer) &
  $[A^{(l)}_s, A^{(l)}_{s-1}]$ (step--step) \\
Holonomy &
  $\mathcal{K}^{(l)}_t$ on fixed grid &
  $\mathcal{K}^{(l)}_s$ at expanding frontier \\
\bottomrule
\end{tabular}
\end{center}

\begin{keyidea}
\textbf{Three geometric regimes of generation.}

The three prompts illustrate three qualitatively different frontier
geometries:

\begin{enumerate}[nosep]
\item \textbf{Retrieval} (factual): high, stable frontier angles
  and curvature; moderate commutator activity --- the model
  confidently retrieves stored knowledge.
\item \textbf{Reasoning} (deductive): lower angles and curvature
  but highest plane drift --- the model makes careful, continuously
  reorienting rotations through a logical chain.
\item \textbf{Adversarial} (repetition collapse): periodic
  patterns in all quantities with period~9 --- the frontier traces
  a fixed orbit, the geometric signature of a degenerate
  generation.
\end{enumerate}
\end{keyidea}

\begin{tryit}
\textbf{Notebook: Chapter 15.}
The companion notebook
\texttt{ch15\_decode\_frontier.ipynb} walks through the full
pipeline: generating with frontier extraction, computing angles and
bivectors, identifying commutator spikes, measuring plane drift,
and computing frontier holonomy.

\medskip
\textbf{Exercises.}

\begin{enumerate}[nosep]
\item Run the frontier pipeline on a creative-writing prompt
  (e.g., ``Once upon a time in a land far away'') with
  temperature~$> 0$.  How does sampling affect frontier angle
  stability compared to greedy decoding?

\item Pick a layer with high commutator activity (e.g., layer~25).
  Plot the full $k \times k$ Cayley bivector matrix at two
  consecutive steps --- one where the commutator is large and one
  where it is small.  How do the sparsity patterns differ?

\item Implement a simple repetition detector: flag a generation as
  ``collapsed'' when the frontier angle autocorrelation at lag~$P$
  exceeds $0.9$ for some period $P < 20$.  Test it on the
  Adversarial prompt and on a non-repetitive generation.

\item Compare frontier holonomy between Qwen2.5-7B and a smaller
  model (e.g., Qwen2.5-1.5B).  Does the smaller model show
  higher frontier curvature, lower, or simply different spatial
  patterns?
\end{enumerate}
\end{tryit}


% в•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђ
\chapter{Capacity and Quality at the Decode Frontier}
\label{chap:frontier-capacity}
% в•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђв•ђ

\begin{whycare}
Chapter~\ref{chap:frontier} described the frontier in terms of
angles, bivectors, commutators, and curvature --- all
\emph{descriptive} quantities.  They tell us \emph{what} happens
geometrically at each decode step.
But they do not tell us \emph{why} some generations are coherent
and others collapse.
This chapter brings the \textbf{capacity} and \textbf{quality}
diagnostics from Part~\ref{part:curvature} to the frontier,
providing the tools to distinguish healthy generation from
degenerate generation on purely geometric grounds.
\end{whycare}


% в”Ђв”Ђ 16.1 в”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђ
\section{Why descriptive geometry is not enough}
\label{sec:cap-motivation}

Chapter~\ref{chap:frontier} established three qualitative
regimes: Retrieval (stable frontier), Reasoning (drifting planes),
and Adversarial (periodic orbit).  All three peak at layer~25
and have similar frontier angle ranges.  The descriptive quantities
alone cannot answer:

\begin{enumerate}[nosep]
\item How much \textbf{computational capacity} does the model use
  at each decode step?
\item Is that capacity distributed across many layers or
  concentrated in a few?
\item Does capacity grow, plateau, or cycle during generation?
\item Can we detect repetition collapse from capacity alone,
  without looking at the tokens?
\end{enumerate}

These are the questions that capacity, grade decomposition, and
quality scores will answer.

\begin{transformer}
In Part~\ref{part:curvature}, Chapters~\ref{chap:capacity}
and~\ref{chap:dependency} defined capacity $C_{\mathrm{acc}}$ as
the accumulated pairwise non-commutativity of bivector generators
across layers.  That was a single number per prompt.  At the
frontier, we compute $C_{\mathrm{acc}}(s)$ at every decode step,
giving a \emph{capacity time series} that tracks how the model's
computational investment evolves during generation.
\end{transformer}


% в”Ђв”Ђ 16.2 в”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђ
\section{Grade decomposition at the frontier}
\label{sec:cap-grade}

Before measuring capacity, we first ask: at each layer transition,
how much of the transformation is \textbf{rotation} (grade-2)
versus \textbf{stretching} (grade-0)?

Recall from Chapter~\ref{chap:versor} that the versor decomposition
$T = RP$ splits a layer operator into a rotor $R$ and a metric
factor $P$.  At the frontier, we approximate this with the
\textbf{directional flow ratio}:
\[
  \boxed{%
    \rho^{(l)}_s
    = \frac{\|A^{(l)}_s\|_F}{\|S^{(l)}_s\|_F + \epsilon}
  }
\]

where $S$ and $A$ are the symmetric and skew-symmetric parts of
the transition operator $M^{(l)}_s = \hat{H}^{(l+1)}_s
\bigl(\hat{H}^{(l)}_s\bigr)^\top / \|\hat{H}^{(l)}_s\|^2$.

When $\rho \gg 1$, the layer is rotation-dominated; when
$\rho \ll 1$, it is stretching-dominated.

\paragraph{What the data shows.}

\begin{figure}[ht]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=\linewidth]{figures_ga_learning/ch16_grade_ratio_3prompts.pdf}
\caption{\textbf{Directional flow ratio heatmaps}
($\rho^{(l)}_s$, layers $\times$ decode steps).
Warm colours indicate rotation-dominated transitions; cool colours
indicate stretching-dominated ones.
All three prompts show a consistent layer profile: early and
late layers are more rotation-dominated, while middle layers
have lower flow ratios.}
\label{fig:grade-ratio}
\end{figure}

\begin{finding}
Mean flow ratios: $0.43$ (Retrieval), $0.31$ (Reasoning), $0.40$
(Adversarial).  All are below~1, meaning frontier transitions are
predominantly metric-driven (stretching/compression) rather than
rotation-driven.

\textbf{Interpretation.}
The Reasoning prompt has the lowest flow ratio, consistent with its
lower frontier angles from Chapter~\ref{chap:frontier}.  The model
applies smaller, more metric-dominated transformations when reasoning
through a logical chain.
The Retrieval prompt has the highest flow ratio, indicating more
rotational activity during factual retrieval.
\end{finding}

\begin{gabox}
\textbf{Grade decomposition and the versor.}
The flow ratio $\rho$ is a lightweight proxy for the full versor
decomposition $T = RP$.  The grade-0 norm $\|S\|$ corresponds to
the metric factor $P$; the grade-2 norm $\|A\|$ corresponds to the
rotor $R$.  At the frontier, where we process $\sim\!26 \times 51$
transitions, the proxy avoids the costly polar decomposition and
matrix logarithm while preserving the essential grade
structure.
\end{gabox}


% в”Ђв”Ђ 16.3 в”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђ
\section{Frontier capacity: accumulated non-commutativity}
\label{sec:cap-frontier}

With a Cayley bivector $A^{(l)}_s$ at every layer transition
and decode step (Chapter~\ref{chap:frontier}), we define the
\textbf{frontier capacity} at step~$s$:

\[
  \boxed{%
    C_{\mathrm{acc}}(s)
    = \sum_{i < j} \bigl\|[A^{(i)}_s,\, A^{(j)}_s]\bigr\|_F
  }
\]

This is the decode-step analogue of the static
$C_{\mathrm{acc}}$ from Chapter~\ref{chap:capacity}: the
accumulated pairwise non-commutativity of all layer bivectors at a
single decode step.  A high $C_{\mathrm{acc}}(s)$ means the model
uses many geometrically distinct rotation planes across layers at
step~$s$; a low value means layers are approximately co-planar.

\paragraph{Incremental capacity.}
The step-to-step change $\Delta C(s) = C_{\mathrm{acc}}(s) -
C_{\mathrm{acc}}(s-1)$ measures how much the model's geometric
complexity changes with each new token.

\begin{figure}[ht]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=\linewidth]{figures_ga_learning/ch16_frontier_capacity_3prompts.pdf}
\caption{\textbf{Frontier capacity} $C_{\mathrm{acc}}(s)$ across
decode steps.  Retrieval has the highest mean capacity ($23.1$);
Reasoning has the lowest ($13.1$); Adversarial starts high but
trends downward as repetition sets in.}
\label{fig:frontier-capacity}
\end{figure}

\begin{finding}
The three prompts show qualitatively different capacity dynamics:

\begin{itemize}[nosep]
\item \textbf{Retrieval} ($\bar{C} = 23.1$):
  high and roughly stable --- the model maintains a rich geometric
  palette as it retrieves factual content.
\item \textbf{Reasoning} ($\bar{C} = 13.1$):
  lower capacity, reflecting fewer geometrically distinct planes
  during deductive reasoning.  The model uses a simpler rotation
  structure.
\item \textbf{Adversarial} ($\bar{C} = 21.0$, trending down):
  starts at retrieval-level capacity but decays as the model
  enters repetition.  The declining $C_{\mathrm{acc}}(s)$ signals
  that the model is \emph{losing} geometric complexity ---
  collapsing into fewer distinct planes.
\end{itemize}
\end{finding}

\begin{figure}[ht]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=\linewidth]{figures_ga_learning/ch16_delta_capacity_3prompts.pdf}
\caption{\textbf{Incremental capacity} $\Delta C(s)$ at each
decode step, annotated with generated tokens.
Positive spikes indicate steps where the model recruits new
geometric planes; negative spikes indicate simplification.
The Adversarial prompt shows periodic oscillations matching
its 9-token repetition cycle.}
\label{fig:delta-capacity}
\end{figure}

\begin{analogy}
Think of an orchestra.  Capacity is the number of
different instruments playing at once.  Retrieval is a full
orchestra maintaining rich instrumentation.  Reasoning is a
string quartet --- fewer instruments, but each one essential.
Repetition collapse is the orchestra gradually reducing to a
single instrument repeating the same phrase.
\end{analogy}


% в”Ђв”Ђ 16.4 в”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђ
\section{Effective rank of frontier capacity}
\label{sec:cap-erank}

$C_{\mathrm{acc}}(s)$ measures the \emph{total} non-commutativity
but not how it is \emph{distributed} across layer pairs.
To measure this, define the \textbf{effective rank}:

\[
  \boxed{%
    \nu(s) = \exp\!\Bigl(-\sum_l p_l(s) \log p_l(s)\Bigr)
  }
\]

where $p_l(s) = c_l(s) / \sum_{l'} c_{l'}(s)$ and $c_l(s)$ is
layer~$l$'s contribution to $C_{\mathrm{acc}}(s)$ (sum of its
row in the pairwise commutator matrix, excluding the diagonal).

The effective rank $\nu(s)$ ranges from~1 (all capacity in one
layer) to $n_{\text{trans}}$ (uniformly distributed).  It is the
exponential of the Shannon entropy of the layer contribution
distribution.

\begin{figure}[ht]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=\linewidth]{figures_ga_learning/ch16_effective_rank_3prompts.pdf}
\caption{\textbf{Effective rank} $\nu(s)$ of frontier capacity.
All three prompts have high effective rank ($\approx 24\text{--}25$
out of 26~transitions), meaning capacity is distributed across
almost all layers rather than concentrated.}
\label{fig:erank}
\end{figure}

\begin{finding}
Effective rank is high for all prompts: $24.6$ (Retrieval), $24.8$
(Reasoning), $25.0$ (Adversarial).  With 26 layer transitions,
these values indicate near-uniform distribution of capacity.

\textbf{Interpretation.}
Unlike the frontier angle (which peaks at layer~25), capacity is
\emph{spread} across the full depth of the network.  No single
layer dominates the non-commutativity budget.  This is consistent
with the BCH perspective from Chapter~\ref{chap:commutators}:
capacity arises from the interaction of many layers rotating in
different planes, not from any one layer's rotation.
\end{finding}

\begin{keyidea}
The effective rank $\nu$ complements $C_{\mathrm{acc}}$ in the
same way that the condition number complements the singular-value
sum.  Two models can have identical total capacity but very
different effective ranks.  A model with $\nu \approx n$ uses
all its layers for geometric computation; a model with
$\nu \approx 1$ concentrates its budget in a single layer pair.
For generation quality, high $\nu$ suggests robust,
distributed computation that is harder to disrupt.
\end{keyidea}


% в”Ђв”Ђ 16.5 в”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђ
\section{Principal planes of frontier bivectors}
\label{sec:cap-planes}

Chapter~\ref{chap:frontier} measured plane drift via the Frobenius
cosine of successive Cayley bivectors.  Here we go deeper: at
selected layers, extract the \textbf{dominant eigenplane} of each
Cayley bivector and track how that plane evolves across decode
steps.

For a skew-symmetric matrix $A$, the eigenvalues come in
conjugate pairs $\pm i\sigma_j$.  The dominant pair defines the
principal plane of rotation; we track its \textbf{stability} as the
cosine between successive bivector matrices at each layer:
\[
  \text{similarity}(s)
  = \frac{|\langle A^{(l)}_s, A^{(l)}_{s-1}\rangle_F|}
         {\|A^{(l)}_s\|_F\;\|A^{(l)}_{s-1}\|_F}
\]

\begin{figure}[ht]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=\linewidth]{figures_ga_learning/ch16_principal_planes_3prompts.pdf}
\caption{\textbf{Principal plane stability} across decode steps
at five selected layers.  Low similarity means the rotation plane
changed between consecutive steps.  The Adversarial prompt shows
periodic plane recurrence matching its 9-token cycle.}
\label{fig:principal-planes}
\end{figure}

\begin{finding}
Plane stability is generally low (below~0.5 at most layers),
confirming Chapter~\ref{chap:frontier}'s finding that rotation
planes are not preserved across decode steps.

The Adversarial prompt is distinctive: its plane similarity shows
periodic peaks, indicating the model returns to the \emph{same}
set of rotation planes at corresponding positions within each
repetition cycle.  This is the geometric fingerprint of a fixed
orbit --- the same planes recur with 9-step periodicity.
\end{finding}


% в”Ђв”Ђ 16.6 в”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђ
\section{Curvature direction at the frontier}
\label{sec:cap-curv-direction}

Chapter~\ref{chap:frontier} computed scalar frontier curvature
$\mathcal{K}^{(l)}_s$.  Scalar curvature answers \emph{how much} the
grid is curved; it does not answer \emph{in which direction}.
The full holonomy rotor (Chapter~\ref{chap:holonomy}) carries a
bivector that names the curvature plane.

Here we track the \textbf{curvature profile similarity}: at each
decode step, the curvature across layers forms a vector
$\mathcal{K}_s = [\mathcal{K}^{(1)}_s, \ldots, \mathcal{K}^{(L-1)}_s]$.  The
cosine between successive profiles measures how stable the
curvature \emph{pattern} is:
\[
  \text{curv\_sim}(s) = \frac{\mathcal{K}_s \cdot \mathcal{K}_{s-1}}
  {\|\mathcal{K}_s\|\;\|\mathcal{K}_{s-1}\|}
\]

\begin{figure}[ht]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=\linewidth]{figures_ga_learning/ch16_curvature_direction_3prompts.pdf}
\caption{\textbf{Curvature profile similarity} across decode steps.
High values indicate that the curvature is distributed across
layers in the same pattern as the previous step.
All three prompts show high similarity ($> 0.9$), meaning the
curvature pattern is remarkably stable even as individual
curvature values change.}
\label{fig:curv-direction}
\end{figure}

\begin{finding}
Curvature profile similarity is consistently high ($> 0.9$) for
all three prompts.  This means:

\begin{itemize}[nosep]
\item The \emph{which-layers-are-curved} pattern is stable across
  generation, even as the \emph{amount} of curvature varies.
\item The model has a fixed ``curvature architecture'' --- certain
  layers are always high-curvature and others always low ---
  regardless of the specific content being generated.
\item This stability justifies Chapter~\ref{chap:frontier}'s
  finding that layer~25 is always the peak: the curvature pattern
  is a structural property of the model, not a content-dependent
  one.
\end{itemize}
\end{finding}


% в”Ђв”Ђ 16.7 в”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђ
\section{Geometric quality scores}
\label{sec:cap-quality}

We now synthesize the frontier quantities into five
\textbf{geometric quality scores} that characterise a generation
without looking at the tokens:

\begin{center}
\renewcommand{\arraystretch}{1.4}\small
\begin{tabular}{@{}L{3cm} L{5cm} L{4cm}@{}}
\toprule
\textbf{Score} & \textbf{Definition} & \textbf{Interpretation} \\
\midrule
Capacity growth rate &
  Slope of $C_{\mathrm{acc}}(s)$ over steps &
  Positive = enriching; negative = simplifying \\
Capacity periodicity &
  Max autocorrelation of $\Delta C(s)$ for
  lag $\in [2, 20]$ &
  High ($> 0.4$) = periodic/repetition \\
Erank trend &
  Slope of $\nu(s)$ over steps &
  Negative = capacity concentrating \\
Plane diversity &
  Mean plane drift from Ch.~\ref{chap:frontier} &
  High = geometrically novel tokens \\
Curvature accel. &
  Mean $|\ddot{K}(s)|$ where
  $K = \sum_l \mathcal{K}^{(l)}$ &
  Zero = steady state (repetition) \\
\bottomrule
\end{tabular}
\end{center}

\begin{figure}[ht]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=\linewidth]{figures_ga_learning/ch16_quality_scores_3prompts.pdf}
\caption{\textbf{Geometric quality scores} for the three prompts.
The Adversarial prompt has the highest capacity periodicity
($0.47$, exceeding the $0.4$ threshold), flagging it as
repetitive without any string matching.
Reasoning has the highest plane diversity ($0.72$).}
\label{fig:quality-scores}
\end{figure}

\begin{finding}
The quality scores for the three prompts:

\medskip
\begin{center}
\renewcommand{\arraystretch}{1.3}\small
\begin{tabular}{@{}l r r r@{}}
\toprule
\textbf{Score} & \textbf{Retrieval} & \textbf{Reasoning} &
  \textbf{Adversarial} \\
\midrule
Cap.\ growth rate & $-0.12$ & $-0.01$ & $-0.16$ \\
Cap.\ periodicity & $0.23$ & $0.26$ & $\mathbf{0.47}$ \\
Erank trend ($\times 10^2$) & $0.28$ & $0.86$ & $0.76$ \\
Plane diversity & $0.66$ & $\mathbf{0.72}$ & $0.68$ \\
Curv.\ acceleration & $0.18$ & $0.14$ & $0.13$ \\
\bottomrule
\end{tabular}
\end{center}

\medskip
\textbf{Key observations:}

\begin{itemize}[nosep]
\item \textbf{Capacity periodicity} cleanly separates the
  Adversarial prompt ($0.47$) from the non-repetitive prompts
  ($0.23$, $0.26$).  A threshold of $0.4$ would correctly flag
  only the repetitive generation.
\item All three prompts have slightly \emph{negative} capacity
  growth rates, suggesting that initial tokens require more
  geometric complexity than later ones.
\item Reasoning has the highest plane diversity ($0.72$),
  consistent with each deductive step requiring a distinct
  geometric direction.
\end{itemize}
\end{finding}

\begin{keyidea}
\textbf{Geometric repetition detection.}
The capacity periodicity score provides a token-free method for
detecting repetition collapse: compute $\Delta C(s)$ at each
decode step, then check the autocorrelation.  If the
maximum autocorrelation for any lag $P \in [2, 20]$ exceeds a
threshold (e.g., $0.4$), flag the generation as repetitive.

This criterion is:
\begin{enumerate}[nosep]
\item \textbf{Token-free:} it operates on the geometry, not the
  output string.
\item \textbf{Early:} it can detect periodicity after
  $\sim\!2P$ steps, before the repetition becomes obvious in the
  text.
\item \textbf{Model-agnostic:} the same threshold works across
  prompts because the geometry is normalised by the whitening
  metric.
\end{enumerate}
\end{keyidea}


% в”Ђв”Ђ 16.8 в”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђ
\section{Case study: the three prompts revisited}
\label{sec:cap-case}

\begin{figure}[ht]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=\linewidth]{figures_ga_learning/ch16_capacity_summary_3prompts.pdf}
\caption{\textbf{Frontier capacity summary.}
\textbf{(a)}~Accumulated capacity $C_{\mathrm{acc}}(s)$.
\textbf{(b)}~Effective rank $\nu(s)$.
\textbf{(c)}~Layer-averaged flow ratio.
\textbf{(d)}~Cumulative capacity growth.
The three prompts separate cleanly: Retrieval is high and stable,
Reasoning is low and steady, Adversarial declines into periodic
cycling.}
\label{fig:capacity-summary}
\end{figure}

Combining all Chapter~16 diagnostics with the descriptive
quantities from Chapter~\ref{chap:frontier}, the three prompts
form three distinct geometric regimes:

\begin{description}[nosep, leftmargin=1.2cm]
\item[Retrieval] (\emph{rich, stable frontier}):
  High capacity ($\bar{C} = 23.1$), high flow ratio ($\rho = 0.43$),
  moderate plane diversity ($0.66$), low periodicity ($0.23$).
  The model maintains a geometrically rich palette and uses it
  steadily to retrieve factual content.

\item[Reasoning] (\emph{lean, diversifying frontier}):
  Low capacity ($\bar{C} = 13.1$), low flow ratio ($\rho = 0.31$),
  highest plane diversity ($0.72$), rising effective rank.
  The model uses fewer geometric resources but continuously
  reorients --- each step in the deductive chain requires a new
  direction.

\item[Adversarial] (\emph{decaying, periodic frontier}):
  Initially high capacity ($\bar{C} = 21.0$), declining over time,
  highest periodicity ($0.47$).  The model starts with rich
  geometry but collapses into a 9-step orbit, losing capacity and
  recycling the same rotation planes.
\end{description}

\begin{pitfall}
The quality scores developed here are validated on three prompts
from one model (Qwen2.5-7B).  The specific thresholds (e.g.,
$0.4$ for periodicity) may need calibration for different model
families or generation strategies (e.g., beam search, nucleus
sampling).  The \emph{geometric structure} of the scores ---
capacity periodicity detecting repetition, plane diversity
reflecting novelty --- should be robust, but the numerical
cutoffs are empirical.
\end{pitfall}


% в”Ђв”Ђ 16.9 в”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђ
\section{Code: frontier capacity pipeline}
\label{sec:cap-code}

The following code builds on Chapter~\ref{chap:frontier}'s
pipeline, adding the Chapter~16 computations:

\begin{lstlisting}[caption={Frontier capacity pipeline}]
from layer_time_ga import (
    generate_with_frontier,
    whiten_frontier,
    compute_frontier_ga,
    frontier_holonomy,
    frontier_grade_profile,
    frontier_capacity,
    frontier_principal_planes,
    frontier_quality_scores,
)

# ... (load model, generate, whiten as in Chapter 15) ...

# Grade decomposition
grade = frontier_grade_profile(result, skip_first=True)
# grade.flow_ratio: (26, 51) directional flow ratio

# Frontier capacity
ga = compute_frontier_ga(result, skip_first=True)
cap = frontier_capacity(ga)
# cap.C_acc:  (51,) accumulated capacity per step
# cap.erank:  (51,) effective rank per step
# cap.delta_C: (50,) incremental capacity

# Principal planes at selected layers
planes = frontier_principal_planes(ga, n_planes=1)

# Quality scores
holo = frontier_holonomy(result, device="cuda")
qs = frontier_quality_scores(ga, cap, holo)
print(f"Periodicity: {qs.capacity_periodicity:.3f}")
print(f"Repetition: {qs.capacity_periodicity > 0.4}")
\end{lstlisting}

\paragraph{Performance.}
The capacity computation is dominated by the pairwise commutators:
$O(n_{\text{trans}}^2 \cdot k^2)$ per step.  For $n = 26$
transitions and $k = 256$, this is $\sim\!22$M operations per step,
or $\sim\!1$B for 51 steps --- fast in numpy.
The principal plane extraction (eigendecomposition of
$256 \times 256$ skew-symmetric matrices) is the most expensive
per-call operation but only runs at a few selected layers.
The full Chapter~16 pipeline adds under 10~minutes to the
Chapter~15 pipeline.


% в”Ђв”Ђ 16.10 в”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђв”Ђ
\section{Summary and bridge to the next chapter}
\label{sec:cap-summary}

This chapter added a \textbf{diagnostic layer} on top of the
descriptive frontier geometry from Chapter~\ref{chap:frontier}:

\begin{center}
\renewcommand{\arraystretch}{1.4}\small
\begin{tabular}{@{}L{3.2cm} L{4.5cm} L{4.5cm}@{}}
\toprule
\textbf{Quantity} & \textbf{Static (Ch.\,11--12)} &
  \textbf{Frontier (this chapter)} \\
\midrule
Capacity &
  $C_{\mathrm{acc}}$, one scalar &
  $C_{\mathrm{acc}}(s)$, time series \\
Effective rank &
  $\nu$, one scalar &
  $\nu(s)$, time series \\
Grade decomp. &
  $\rho^{(l)}$, per layer &
  $\rho^{(l)}_s$, per layer per step \\
Quality score &
  (not available) &
  Five diagnostic scores per generation \\
\bottomrule
\end{tabular}
\end{center}

\begin{keyidea}
\textbf{Three levels of frontier analysis.}

Chapters~\ref{chap:frontier} and~\ref{chap:frontier-capacity}
provide three complementary levels:

\begin{enumerate}[nosep]
\item \textbf{Descriptive} (Ch.~\ref{chap:frontier}):
  angle, bivector, commutator, drift, holonomy --- what happens at
  each step.
\item \textbf{Diagnostic} (this chapter):
  capacity, effective rank, grade ratio, quality scores --- why
  some generations are healthy and others collapse.
\item \textbf{Predictive} (future work):
  using capacity trends and periodicity to \emph{detect} and
  potentially \emph{intervene} in generation failures before they
  manifest in the output text.
\end{enumerate}
\end{keyidea}

\begin{tryit}
\textbf{Notebook: Chapter 16.}
The companion notebook
\texttt{ch16\_frontier\_capacity.ipynb} walks through the full
capacity pipeline, from grade decomposition to quality scores.

\medskip
\textbf{Exercises.}

\begin{enumerate}[nosep]
\item Compute $C_{\mathrm{acc}}(s)$ for the Adversarial prompt
  and fit a periodic model $C_{\mathrm{acc}}(s) \approx \alpha +
  \beta \cos(2\pi s / P)$.  What period $P$ gives the best fit?
  Does it match the 9-token repetition cycle?

\item Run the quality score pipeline on a generation with
  temperature~$> 0$ (e.g., temperature~$= 0.8$).  Does
  sampling affect capacity periodicity?  Does it reduce or
  increase effective rank?

\item Implement an early stopping criterion: halt generation
  when the capacity periodicity score computed on the last
  $2P_{\max}$ steps exceeds~$0.4$.  Test on 5~different prompts.
  How early can the detector identify repetition compared to a
  string-matching detector?

\item Compare the frontier capacity profile between Qwen2.5-7B and
  a larger model (e.g., Qwen2.5-32B).  Does the larger model use
  more capacity per step, or does it use similar capacity more
  efficiently (higher effective rank)?
\end{enumerate}
\end{tryit}
